Ruling on Borrowing Land for Building or Planting: Permissibility, Revocation, and Compensation
Presents ruling and details on lender's revocation and compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on borrowing land for building or planting, explain the lender's right to revoke, and distinguish the compensation owed when a time limit is set versus not set.

Core Idea
Borrowing land for building is permissible but revocable; compensation depends on whether a time was promised.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar on the 'ariyah (loan) of land for constructing buildings or planting trees. The text establishes that such a loan is valid by analogy to lease (ijarah), grants the lender the right to revoke, and specifies when the borrower is entitled to compensation. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the measure of compensation and the distinction between planting trees and cultivating crops.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal nature
Read the opening sentence: borrowing land for building/planting is permissible (ja'iz). Note that this is an 'ariyah contract involving a known usufruct.
The act is not ibadah but a contract of gratuitous loan.
2
Understand the dalil for permissibility
The text provides an analogy to ijarah: since a known usufruct can be owned via lease, it can also be owned via 'ariyah. Identify this as qiyas with a known illah.
The dalil is aqli (rational analogy), not naqli.
3
Distinguish between the two scenarios: no time limit vs. time limit
If no time is set, the borrower is a non-deceived volunteer (mu'tar) and bears removal costs. If a time is set and the lender revokes early, the lender must compensate because the borrower is deceived (maghrur).
The key is whether the borrower relied on a promise (time limit).
4
Analyze the two views on compensation
Al-Quduri holds: lender pays the decrease in value due to removal. Al-Hakim al-Shahid holds: lender pays full value and becomes owner, or borrower may remove. Compare the rationales.
Al-Quduri's view is from the Mukhtasar (zahir al-riwayah); al-Hakim's is a nawadir position.
5
Note the exception for cultivation versus planting
For annual crops (zira'ah), the borrower may stay until harvest because the crop has a known end; for trees (ghars), there is no known end, so removal is compelled to prevent harm to the landowner.
This distinction flows from the principle of daf' al-darar (prevention of harm).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا اسْتَعَارَ أَرْضُ لِيَبْنِيَ فِيْهَا أَوْ لِيَغْرَسَ جَازَ وَلِلْمُعِيرِ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ فِيهَا وَيُكَلَّفَهُ قَلْعَ الْبِنَاءِ وَالْغَرْسُ أَمَّا الرُّجُوعُ فَلِمَا بَيَّنَّا وَأَمَّا الْجَوَازُ فَلَانَّهَا مَنْفَعَةٌ مَعْلُومَةٌ تُمْلَكُ بِالْإِجَارَةِ فَكَذَا بِالْإِعَارَةِ وَإِذَا صَحَ الرُّجُوعُ بَقِيَ الْمُسْتَعِيرُ شَاغِلًا أَرْضَ الْمُعِيرِ فَيُكَلِّفُ تَفْرِيْغَهَا ثُمَّ إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ وَقَتَ الْعَارِيَةَ فَلَا ضَمَانَ عَلَيْهِ لَانَّ الْمُسْتَعِيْرَ مُعْتَرٌ غَيْرُ مَغْرُورٍ حَيْثُ اعْتَمَدَ إِطْلَاقَ الْعَقْدِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يَسْبِقَ مِنْهُ الْوَعْدُ وَإِنْ كَانَ وَقَتَ الْعَارِيَةَ وَرَجَعَ قَبْلَ الْوَقْتِ صَحَ رُجُوعُهُ لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا وَلَكِنَّهُ يُكْرَهُ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ خَلْفِ الْوَعْدِ وَضَمِنَ الْمُعِيْرُ مَا نَقَصَ الْبِنَاءُ وَالْغَرُسُ بِالْقَلْعِ لِأَنَّهُ مَغْرُورٌ مِنْ جِهَتِهِ حَيْثُ وَقَتَ لَهُ فَالظَّاهِرُ هُوَ الْوَفَاءُ بِالْعَهْدِ فَيَرْجِعُ عَلَيْهِ دَفْعًا لِلضَّرَرِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ كَذَا ذَكَرَهُ الْقُدُورِى فِى الْمُخْتَصَرِ وَذَكَرَ الْحَاكِمُ الشَّهِيدُ أَنَّهُ يَضْمَنُ رَبِّ الْأَرْضِ لِلْمُسْتَعِيْرِ قِيْمَةَ غُرْسِهِ وَبِنَائِهِ وَيَكُونَانِ لَهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ الْمُسْتَعِيرُ أَنْ يَرْفَعَهُمَا وَلَا يُضَمِّنُهُ قِيْمَتَهَا فَيَكُونُ لَهُ ذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ مِلْكُهُ قَالُوا إِذَا كَانَ فِي الْقَلْعِ ضَرَرٌ بِالْأَرْضِ فَالْخِيَارُ إِلَى رَبِّ الْأَرْضِ لِأَنَّهُ صَاحِبُ الْأَصْلِ وَالْمُسْتَعِيْرُ صَاحِبُ تَبَعِ وَالتَّرْجِيحُ بِالْأَصْلِ وَلَوِ اسْتَعَارَهَا لِيَزْرَعَهَا لَمْ يُؤْخَذْ مِنْهُ حَتَّى يُحْصَدَ الزَّرْعَ وَقَتَ أَو لَمْ يُوَقِّتْ لِأَنَّ لَهُ نِهَايَةً مَعْلُومَةً وَفِي التَّرْكِ بِالْأَجْرِ مُرَاعَاةُ الْحَقَّيْنِ بِخِلَافِ الْغَرْسِ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ لَهُ نِهَايَةٌ مَعْلُومَةٌ فَيُقْلَعُ دَفْعًا لِلضَّرَرِ عَنِ الْمَالِكِ
He said: And when one borrows land to build in it or to plant trees, it is permissible, and the lender has the right to revoke it and to require him to remove the building and the planting. As for the right to revoke, it is for what we have explained. As for permissibility, it is because it is a known usufruct that can be owned by ijarah (lease), so also by 'ariyah. And when the revocation is valid, the borrower remains occupying the lender's land, so he is required to vacate it. Then if he did not fix a time for the 'ariyah, there is no compensation (daman) upon him because the borrower is a non-deceived volunteer (mu'tar ghayr maghrur) since he relied on the absolute contract without any prior promise. And if he fixed a time and then revoked before the time, his revocation is valid due to what we mentioned, but it is disliked (makruh) because it breaches the promise. And the lender is liable for the decrease in the building and planting due to removal, because the borrower is deceived (maghrur) by him since he specified a time, so the apparent expectation is fulfillment of the covenant, so he (the lender) compensates him to prevent harm to himself. Thus al-Quduri mentioned in al-Mukhtasar. And al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned that the landowner must pay the borrower the value of his planting and building, and they become the property of the landowner, unless the borrower wishes to remove them, and he is not liable for their value; then that is allowed because they are his property. They said: If removal causes harm to the land, then the choice is with the landowner because he is the owner of the origin (asl) and the borrower is the owner of the accessory (taba'), and preference is given to the origin. And if he borrowed it for cultivation, he is not made to leave until the crop is harvested, whether he fixed a time or not, because the crop has a known end, and letting it remain with a fee (ujr) observes both rights, unlike planting because it has no known end, so it is removed to prevent harm to the owner.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the loan ('ariyah) of land for building or planting. The author states that such an 'ariyah is valid because the usufruct is known (ma'lumah) and can be owned through ijarah (lease), so it can also be owned through 'ariyah. The lender has the right to revoke the 'ariyah at any time, and the borrower must remove any structures. However, the liability for damages depends on whether a time limit was set. If no time was set, the borrower is a non-deceived volunteer and bears the cost of removal. If a time was set and the lender revokes early, it is disliked (makruh) and the lender must compensate the borrower for the decrease in value due to removal (i.e., the diminution in the building/planting). Two positions are then cited: al-Quduri (in al-Mukhtasar) holds that the lender compensates only for the decrease caused by removal. Al-Hakim al-Shahid holds that the landowner must pay the full value of the building/planting and becomes their owner, unless the borrower prefers to remove them. Additionally, if removal would damage the land, the choice belongs to the landowner (due to his ownership of the origin). The passage concludes with a distinction: if the land was borrowed for cultivation (zira'ah), the crop cannot be removed until harvest, because it has a known end and letting it remain with a fee balances both rights; but planting (ghirs) has no known end, so it must be removed to prevent harm to the owner. The authority weight is from the zahir al-riwayah as presented by al-Quduri, with al-Hakim al-Shahid's view as a differing opinion (possibly from the nawadir).

Key Terms
مُعْتَرّ (mu‘tar): A person who makes use of something voluntarily, without being deceived or promised anything.
مَغْرُور (maghrūr): A person who is deceived or misled by another's promise, thus entitled to compensation for reliance.
الغَرْس (al-ghars): Planting trees (not annual crops) in land; considered a permanent improvement with no known termination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Quduri in al-Mukhtasar, if a lender revokes a time-bound 'ariyah of land for building, what must the lender pay?
A) Nothing, because 'ariyah is revocable at will.
B) The full value of the building.
C) The decrease in value of the building caused by removal.
D) The cost of removing the building.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility and revocation of 'ariyah are clear, but compensation depends on whether the borrower relied on a promise of time.

Borrowing Land for Building or Planting: Permissibility and the Lender's Right to Revoke
Hanafi ruling on borrowing land for building/planting with proof by qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on borrowing land for building or planting and explain the lender's right to revoke, using qiyas to ijarah.

Core Idea
Borrowing land for building or planting is valid, but the lender can revoke anytime and demand removal.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a practical scenario: someone borrows land to build a structure or plant trees. The ruling balances the borrower's right to use the land with the lender's ownership. We'll examine the permissibility and the lender's right to revoke, along with the proof by analogy to lease contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the first sentence: the borrower borrows land to build or plant. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz).
The act is borrowing (isti'arah) for building or planting.
2
Determine the lender's right
The text explicitly states: 'wa lil-mu'iri an yarji'a' – the lender has the right to revoke. The borrower must then remove the structures or trees (qal').
This right is unconditional; the lender can revoke at any time.
3
Analyze the proof for permissibility
The author gives a qiyas: the benefit of building/planting is a known benefit (manfa'ah ma'lumah) that can be owned through ijarah (lease). By analogy, it can also be obtained through 'ariyah (loan).
The illah (effective cause) is that both contracts involve the transfer of a known benefit for a known purpose.
4
Recognize the opposing view
Ibn Abi Layla held that improvements become the lender's property with compensation. The Hanafi school rejects this, maintaining the borrower's duty to remove.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf, but the mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا اسْتَعَارَ أَرْضُ لِيَبْنِيَ فِيْهَا أَوْ لِيَغْرَسَ جَازَ وَلِلْمُعِيرِ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ فِيهَا وَيُكَلَّفَهُ قَلْعَ الْبِنَاءِ وَالْغَرْسُ أَمَّا الرُّجُوعُ فَلِمَا بَيَّنَّا وَأَمَّا الْجَوَازُ فَلَانَّهَا مَنْفَعَةٌ مَعْلُومَةٌ تُمْلَكُ بِالْإِجَارَةِ فَكَذَا بِالْإِعَارَةِ
He said: And when one borrows land to build in it or to plant trees, it is permissible. The lender has the right to revoke it and shall be ordered to remove the building and the planting. As for revocation, it is for what we have explained. As for permissibility, it is because this is a known benefit that is owned through lease, so similarly through loan (i'arah).
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah presents the ruling on borrowing land for building or planting. The ruling is that such a loan is valid and permissible (ja'iz). However, the lender (mu'ir) retains the right to revoke the loan at any time and demand that the borrower (musta'ir) remove the structures or trees. The reasoning is twofold: first, the right to revoke is based on the general principle that 'ariyah is a non-binding contract (ja'iz), as previously established. Second, the permissibility of using the land for building or planting is justified by qiyas (analogy) to ijarah (lease): since a known benefit like building or planting can be acquired through a lease contract, it can similarly be acquired through a gratuitous loan. This is an example of qiyas jaliy (clear analogy). The lender's right to revoke is unconditional, and the borrower must comply by vacating the land.

Key Terms
عارية (ʻĀriyah): A contract of gratuitous loan of a chattel (or land) for use, with the obligation to return the specific item.
مستعير (Mustaʻīr): The borrower in an 'ariyah contract – the person receiving the loaned item for use.
معير (Muʻīr): The lender in an 'ariyah contract – the owner who lends the item.
قلع (Qalʻ): The act of removing or uprooting the building or trees from the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (hukm) on borrowing land for planting trees according to this passage?
A) It is not valid because the land is not consumable.
B) It is permissible, and the lender cannot revoke the loan.
C) It is permissible, and the lender may revoke and demand removal.
D) It is invalid unless a rental fee is paid.

Key Takeaway
Borrowing land for building or planting is permissible, but the lender may revoke at any time, forcing the borrower to remove the improvements at his own cost.

Compensation When Lender Revokes Loan Land: Fixed Time vs No Fixed Time
Hanafi rule on compensation for building/trees removal upon loan revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on compensation when a lender revokes a loan of land, distinguish between cases with and without a fixed time, and apply the concepts of mu'tar (self-deceived) and maghrur (deceived by another) to determine liability.

Core Idea
Lender compensates only if a time was fixed and he revokes early; otherwise borrower bears loss as self-deceived.

This lesson examines a detailed ruling from Al-Hidayah on the liability of a lender (mu'ir) when he revokes a loan of land (ariyah) on which the borrower has built or planted. The key factor is whether a specific duration was agreed upon. If no time was fixed, the borrower is considered self-deceived and bears the loss. If a time was fixed and the lender revokes early, he must compensate for the decrease in value caused by removal. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Two Scenarios
The passage deals with revocation of a loan of land where the borrower has made improvements (building or planting). Identify the two scenarios: (1) no fixed time, (2) fixed time with early revocation.
The ruling depends entirely on whether a time (tawqit) was set.
2
Determine Liability in Each Scenario
In scenario 1 (no fixed time): the lender is not liable (la daman). In scenario 2 (fixed time, early revocation): the lender is liable (damana ma naqasa). Note also that the revocation is still valid but makruh in scenario 2.
Liability is for the decrease in value (ma naqasa) due to removal, not the full value of the improvements.
3
Understand the Ta'lil (Reasoning)
The passage provides a legal cause (illa) based on whether the borrower is mu'tar (self-deceived) or maghrur (deceived by another). The borrower who relies on an open-ended contract is mu'tar; one who relies on a fixed term is maghrur because the lender led him to expect the term to be honored.
This distinction justifies the different liability outcomes.
4
Identify the Proof and Authority
The passage cites al-Quduri's al-Mukhtasar as the source of this ruling. The proof is not a direct text but a rational argument based on the concept of deception and the principle that harm should be repelled (daf' al-darar).
The ruling is part of the zahir al-riwayah transmitted by Imam Muhammad.
5
Note the Conflicting View
The text implies a difference with Ibn Abi Layla, who held that the building belongs to the lender and he must pay full value. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on tawqit.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (mukhtalaf fih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا صَحَ الرُّجُوعُ بَقِيَ الْمُسْتَعِيرُ شَاغِلًا أَرْضَ الْمُعِيرِ فَيُكَلِّفُ تَفْرِيْغَهَا ثُمَّ إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ وَقَتَ الْعَارِيَةَ فَلَا ضَمَانَ عَلَيْهِ لَانَّ الْمُسْتَعِيْرَ مُعْتَرٌ غَيْرُ مَغْرُورٍ حَيْثُ اعْتَمَدَ إِطْلَاقَ الْعَقْدِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يَسْبِقَ مِنْهُ الْوَعْدُ وَإِنْ كَانَ وَقَتَ الْعَارِيَةَ وَرَجَعَ قَبْلَ الْوَقْتِ صَحَ رُجُوعُهُ لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا وَلَكِنَّهُ يُكْرَهُ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ خَلْفِ الْوَعْدِ وَضَمِنَ الْمُعِيْرُ مَا نَقَصَ الْبِنَاءُ وَالْغَرُسُ بِالْقَلْعِ لِأَنَّهُ مَغْرُورٌ مِنْ جِهَتِهِ حَيْثُ وَقَتَ لَهُ فَالظَّاهِرُ هُوَ الْوَفَاءُ بِالْعَهْدِ فَيَرْجِعُ عَلَيْهِ دَفْعًا لِلضَّرَرِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ كَذَا ذَكَرَهُ الْقُدُورِى فِى الْمُخْتَصَرِ
And when revocation is valid, the borrower remains occupying the lender's land, so he is ordered to vacate it. Then, if he did not fix a time for the loan, there is no liability on him (the lender) because the borrower is one who deceived himself (mu'tar), not one deceived (maghrur), since he relied on the absolute nature of the contract without any preceding promise from him (the lender). And if he fixed a time for the loan and revoked before the time, his revocation is valid for what we have mentioned, but it is disliked (makruh) because it involves breaking a promise. And the lender is liable for the decrease in value of the building and planting due to removal, because the borrower is one deceived (maghrur) by him, since he fixed a time for him, so the apparent (expectation) is fulfillment of the covenant; thus the borrower recovers from him to repel harm from himself. Thus al-Quduri mentioned it in al-Mukhtasar.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah provides the detailed ruling on compensation (daman) when the lender revokes a loan of land on which the borrower has built or planted. The key distinction is whether a time (tawqit) was fixed for the loan. If no specific time was agreed, the borrower is considered mu'tar (one who deceived himself) because he relied on the open-ended nature of the contract without any promise from the lender. Therefore, when the lender revokes, the lender is not liable for any loss to the building or trees from removal; the borrower bears the loss himself. However, if a fixed duration was agreed upon and the lender revokes before that time expires, the revocation is still valid (the contract remains non-binding), but it is makruh tanzihi (disliked) because it involves breaking a promise (khulf al-wa'd). In this case, the lender is liable (damana) for the decrease in value caused by removal because the borrower is maghrur (deceived) – he was led to believe the loan would last for the specified period. The borrower can recover this loss from the lender. This is the position of al-Quduri in al-Mukhtasar.

Key Terms
ضمان (Ḍamān): Liability or compensation for loss or damage; here, the lender's obligation to pay for the decrease in value caused by removal.
مغتر (Mughtarr): One who is deceived by another, leading to a course of action; here, the borrower who relied on the fixed time set by the lender.
وقت العارية (Waqt al-ʻĀriyah): The fixed time period agreed upon for the loan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when is the lender (mu'ir) liable for the decrease in value of the building caused by removal?
A) When the lender revokes at any time.
B) When no time was fixed for the loan.
C) When a time was fixed and the lender revokes before that time.
D) When the borrower demands compensation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a lender of land is only liable for loss to the borrower's improvements if a fixed term was agreed and broken, because the borrower is then considered deceived (maghrur).

Hakim al-Shahid's View on Compensation and Landowner's Option When Removal Harms Land
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation and landowner's option when removal damages land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on compensation for improvements when a land loan is revoked, distinguish between the views of al-Quduri and al-Hakim al-Shahid, and apply the rule when removal damages the land.

Core Idea
If removing improvements harms the land, the landowner may keep them by paying full value — the original takes precedence over the accessory.

This lesson examines a refinement within the Hanafi school concerning a loan of land for planting or building. When the lender revokes the loan, the borrower must remove his structures or trees. But what if removal damages the land? Al-Hakim al-Shahid offers a different view on compensation, and later scholars give the landowner a choice to pay full value and keep the improvements. Understanding this intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf clarifies the legal principle that the original (land) prevails over the accessory (building/trees).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the transaction
Recognize that a landowner (mu'ir) loaned land to a borrower (musta'ir) for planting trees (ghars) or building (bina'). The loan is revocable, and the lender demands removal.
This is a contract of 'ariyah (loan) for land.
2
State the default ruling from al-Quduri
According to al-Quduri, when the lender revokes, the borrower must remove the improvements, and the lender pays only the decrease in the land's value caused by removal.
This is the baseline ruling before al-Hakim al-Shahid's refinement.
3
Present al-Hakim al-Shahid's contrary view
Al-Hakim al-Shahid holds that the lender must pay the full market value of the improvements, after which the improvements become the lender's property. However, the borrower may choose to remove them and forgo compensation.
This view treats the improvements as owned by the borrower until compensation is paid, but gives the borrower an option.
4
Analyze the refinement when removal harms the land
Later scholars (qalu) add: if removal physically damages the land, the choice (khiyar) shifts to the landowner. He can either pay the full value and keep the improvements or force removal? The text implies he can choose to pay and keep, because the land is the original (asl) and the improvements are accessory (taba'), and the original is preferred.
This protects the landowner's property from harm.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarat al-khilaf)
Under this refinement, if removal would damage the land, the landowner can compel the borrower to accept full value and leave the improvements intact. The borrower cannot insist on removal.
This overrides the borrower's right to remove his own property to prevent harm to the land.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَذَكَرَ الْحَاكِمُ الشَّهِيدُ أَنَّهُ يَضْمَنُ رَبِّ الْأَرْضِ لِلْمُسْتَعِيْرِ قِيْمَةَ غُرْسِهِ وَبِنَائِهِ وَيَكُونَانِ لَهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ الْمُسْتَعِيرُ أَنْ يَرْفَعَهُمَا وَلَا يُضَمِّنُهُ قِيْمَتَهَا فَيَكُونُ لَهُ ذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ مِلْكُهُ قَالُوا إِذَا كَانَ فِي الْقَلْعِ ضَرَرٌ بِالْأَرْضِ فَالْخِيَارُ إِلَى رَبِّ الْأَرْضِ لِأَنَّهُ صَاحِبُ الْأَصْلِ وَالْمُسْتَعِيْرُ صَاحِبُ تَبَعِ وَالتَّرْجِيحُ بِالْأَصْلِ
And al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned that the owner of the land pays to the borrower the value of his planting and building, and they become his (the owner's) unless the borrower wishes to remove them and does not ask him for their value; then that is allowed for him because it is his property. They (the scholars) said: When there is harm to the land from removal, then the choice is with the owner of the land because he is the owner of the original (land) and the borrower is the owner of the accessory (building/trees), and preference is given to the original.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative position from al-Hakim al-Shahid (a prominent Hanafi scholar). According to his view, when the lender revokes the loan of land and demands removal of the building/trees, the lender (owner of the land) must pay the borrower the full market value of the structures and trees, and then becomes their owner. However, the borrower retains the option to remove them himself and forego compensation (since they are his property). This is a departure from the earlier ruling in the same text that only gives compensation for the decrease in value due to removal. The passage then records a further ruling from 'they said' (probably later scholars): if removal (qal') would cause harm to the land itself, the choice (khiyar) lies with the landowner – he can either pay the value and keep the improvements or force removal? The text states the preference is given to the original (asl) over the accessory (taba'), meaning the landowner's right to protect his land takes precedence. The practical effect: if removal damages the land, the landowner may opt to pay the value and keep the building/trees, and the borrower cannot refuse. This is a refinement of the earlier rule, giving the landowner more control.

Key Terms
حاكم شھید (Al-Ḥākim al-Shahīd): Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Marwazi, a Hanafi jurist and hadith scholar, known for his works and martyrdom in 334 AH.
غموس (Ghars): Planting, trees that are planted.
تابع (Tābiʻ): Accessory; here, the building/trees are considered subsidiary to the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rule when removing the building would harm the land?
A) The borrower must remove at his own expense.
B) The landowner has the choice to pay the value and keep the building.
C) The building must remain and the lender pays rent.
D) The matter goes to a judge.

Key Takeaway
When removal of improvements damages the land, the landowner may keep them by paying full value, thereby preventing harm and respecting the precedence of the original over the accessory.

Crops vs Trees on Borrowed Land
Hanafi distinction: crops vs trees on borrowed land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi rulings for crops (zar') and trees (ghars) when a lender revokes a loan of land, and explain the reasoning based on the presence or absence of a known end.

Core Idea
Crops have a known end; trees do not — that defines the borrower's right to remain.

When land is borrowed for planting, the lender can normally revoke at any time. But what happens if crops or trees are already growing? Should the borrower have to clear the land immediately? This passage from al-Hidayah sets out the Hanafi distinction: annual crops are temporary, so the borrower may stay until harvest; trees are permanent, so they must be removed at once. The key is the nature of the planting — temporary versus indefinite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two cases: land borrowed to plant crops (zar') and land used for planting trees (ghars). Note the different rulings for each.
The same principle applies regardless of whether a time was fixed.
2
State the ruling for each
For crops, the lender cannot take the land back until the crop is harvested. For trees, the borrower must remove them immediately upon revocation.
The borrower pays fair rental (ujr al-mithl) for the extra period in the crop case.
3
Identify the ta'lil (reason)
The ruling is based on whether the planting has a known end (nihayah ma'lumah). Crops will naturally die after harvest; trees are indefinite. Thus crops are left to honor both rights, trees are removed to prevent ongoing harm to the owner.
This is a rule of necessity and fairness — not arbitrary.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Given a scenario (e.g., borrower plants wheat vs. apple trees), apply the distinction. Decide: what must the borrower do when the lender revokes?
Check your answer against the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوِ اسْتَعَارَهَا لِيَزْرَعَهَا لَمْ يُؤْخَذْ مِنْهُ حَتَّى يُحْصَدَ الزَّرْعَ وَقَتَ أَو لَمْ يُوَقِّتْ لِأَنَّ لَهُ نِهَايَةً مَعْلُومَةً وَفِي التَّرْكِ بِالْأَجْرِ مُرَاعَاةُ الْحَقَّيْنِ بِخِلَافِ الْغَرْسِ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ لَهُ نِهَايَةٌ مَعْلُومَةٌ فَيُقْلَعُ دَفْعًا لِلضَّرَرِ عَنِ الْمَالِكِ
And if he borrowed it to plant crops, it is not taken from him until the crop is harvested, whether he fixed a time or not, because it has a known end. And in leaving it for a rental payment (bi al-ajr) there is observance of both rights. Contrary to planting trees, because it has no known end, so it is removed to repel harm from the owner.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah provides an important distinction within the loan of land for agricultural purposes. If the land is borrowed specifically for planting seasonal crops (zar'), the land cannot be reclaimed by the lender until the crop is harvested, regardless of whether a fixed time was agreed upon. The reason (ta'lil) is that crops have a known and limited duration (nihayah ma'lumah). By allowing the borrower to keep the land until harvest (with payment of an appropriate rental, ujr al-mithl), both parties' rights are observed: the lender gets compensation for the additional use, and the borrower can harvest his crop. This is in contrast to trees (ghars), which have no naturally limited life; they can persist indefinitely. Therefore, with trees, the borrower is required to remove them immediately to prevent ongoing harm to the lender's ownership. The distinction is based on the nature of the planting: temporary versus permanent.

Key Terms
زرع (Zarʻ): Crops, typically annual plants like wheat, barley, etc., that are harvested within a season.
غرس (Ghars): Planting of trees, which have an indefinite lifespan.
أجر مثل (Ujr al-Mithl): The fair rental value of the land for the additional period the borrower keeps it to harvest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are trees (ghars) treated differently from crops (zar') when the lender revokes a loan of land?
A) Because trees are planted by the borrower himself.
B) Because crops have a known end, while trees do not.
C) Because trees are more valuable.
D) Because the lender prefers trees.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the borrower's right to retain borrowed land after the lender's revocation depends on whether the planting has a natural end: crops may be left until harvest; trees must be removed immediately.

Returning a Borrowed Animal to the Owner's Stable: Istihsan vs. Qiyas
Ruling on liability when borrowed animal is returned to owner's stable or via slave/worker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of istihsan to determine the borrower's liability when returning a borrowed item to the owner's property, distinguishing customary from non-customary returns.

Core Idea
A borrower is not liable if the borrowed item perishes after being returned to the owner's home in the customary way — custom overrides strict analogy.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a practical scenario: you borrow a horse, return it to the owner's stable, and it dies before the owner takes possession. Are you liable? The Hanafi school says no, by istihsan (juristic preference), while other schools hold you liable by qiyas. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning and its conditions, including the role of custom and the distinction between household members and strangers.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and the ikhtilaf
Identify that the Hanafi ruling is non-liability (istihsan), while Shafi'i, Hanbali, and Maliki hold liability (qiyas). This sets up the dispute.
The dispute is not just theoretical; it has practical financial consequences.
2
Examine the qiyas argument for liability
Understand why qiyas demands liability: the animal was not delivered to the owner's person, so it remains in the borrower's responsibility until actual receipt by the owner.
Qiyas here is based on the analogy of the item being destroyed if not properly handed over.
3
Understand the istihsan reasoning based on custom
Analyze the custom ('urf): borrowed animals are typically returned to the owner's home or stable. Since the owner would stable it himself if it were with him, the borrower's action is considered a valid return.
Istihsan operates as a secondary source that overrides qiyas when there is a compelling reason like custom or necessity.
4
Note the exception when the stable is outside the house
If the stable is located outside the owner's residence, the borrower remains liable because the animal would be left unprotected, and that is not customary.
This exception shows that istihsan is not arbitrary but tied to the presence of custom.
5
Extend the principle to returning via agents
Review the rules for returning through a slave or long-term worker (not liable) versus daily worker or stranger (liable). This reinforces the concept of trust-based agency.
The household member is considered an extension of the owner for trust purposes, but a daily wage worker is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله واذا استعار دابته .... الخ کسی نے ایک گھوڑ استعار لیا پھر اس کو مالک کے اصطبل تک واپس کیا اور وہ تلف ہو گیا تو از روئے استحسان ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ قیاساً ضامن ہونا چاہیے۔ کیونکہ اس نے مالک کو واپس نہیں کیا بلکہ ضائع کر دیا۔
امام شافعی ، امام احمد اور اصح قول میں امام مالک اسی کے قائل ہیں۔ وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ عاریت لینے کے بعد مالک کے گھر پہنچادی جاتی ہیں ۔ پس اس نے گھوڑے کو بطریق متعارف سپرد کر دیا۔ نیز اگر وہ گھوڑا مالک کے پاس پہنچا تا تو مالک پھر اس کو اصطبل پہنچا تا لہذا مستغیر کا واپس کرنا صحیح ٹھہرا۔ شیخ تمرتاشی نے ابو سلمہ سے نقل کیا ہے کہ اگر مربط خارج دار ہو تو مستغیر بری الذمہ نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں ظاہر یہی ہے کہ وہاں گھوڑ ابلا محافظ ہوگا۔ وقیل ھذا فی عاد تھم ۔
کسی نے چوپایہ عاریت پر لیا اور اس کو اپنے غلام یا نوکر کے ہاتھ واپس کیا تو ضامن نہ ہوگا
قَالَ وَمَنِ اسْتَعَارَ دَابَّةٌ فَرَدَّهَا مَعَ عَبْدِهِ أَوْ أَجِيْرِهِ لَمْ يَضْمَنْ وَالْمُرَادُ بِالْاجِيْرِانْ يَكُونَ مُسَانَهَةٌ أَوْ مُشَاهَرَةٌ لأَنَّهَا أَمَانَةٌ فَلَهُ أَنْ يَحْفَظَهَا بِيَدِ مَنْ فِى عِمَالِهِ كَمَا فِي الْوَدِيْعَةِ بِخِلَافِ الْآجِيْرِ مُيَا وَمَةٌ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي عَيَالِهِ وَكَذَا إِذَا رَدَّهَا مَعَ عَبْدِ رَبِّ الدَّابَّةِ أَوْ أَجِيْرِهِ لِاَنَّ الْمَالِكَ يَرْضَى بِهِ أَلَا تَرَى انَّهُ لَوْ رَدَّهُ إِلَيْهِ فَهُوَ يَرُدُّهُ إِلَى عَبْدِهِ وَقِيلَ هَذَا فِي الْعَبْدِ الَّذِي يَقُومُ عَلَى الدَّوَاتِ وَقِيلَ فِيهِ وَفِي غَيْرِهِ وَهُوَ الْاصَحُ لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ لَا يَدْفَعُ إِلَيْهِ دَائِمًا يَدْفَعُ إِلَيْهِ أَحْيَانًا وَإِنْ كَانَ رَدَّهَا مَعَ أَجْنَبِي ضَمِنَ وَدَلَّتِ الْمَسْأَلَهُ عَلَى أَنَّ الْمُسْتَعِيْرَ لَا يَمْلِكُ الْإِبْدَاعَ
His statement: 'And when he borrows an animal...' etc. Someone borrowed a horse, then returned it to the owner's stable, and it perished. By istihsan, he is not liable. By qiyas, he should be liable because he did not return it to the owner but rather destroyed it. Imam al-Shafi'i, Imam Ahmad, and in the more correct view Imam Malik hold this. The basis of istihsan: borrowed items are usually delivered to the owner's home. So he delivered the horse in the customary manner. Also, if the horse had reached the owner, the owner would then take it to the stable; hence the borrower's return is valid. Shaykh al-Tamrtashi narrated from Abu Salama: if the stable is outside the house, the borrower is not discharged because it is apparent that the horse would be unprotected there. And it is said this is according to their custom.
If someone borrowed an animal and returned it via his own slave or hired worker, he is not liable.
He said: And whoever borrows an animal and returns it with his slave or his ajir (hired worker) is not liable. The intended meaning of ajir is one hired by the year or month, because it is an amanah (trust), so he may keep it in the hand of someone in his household as in wadi'ah (deposit). This is unlike the daily wage worker (ajir murakhab? actually 'ajir miyaumah') because he is not in his household. Likewise, if he returns it with the owner's slave or ajir, because the owner is pleased with that. Do you not see that if he returned it to him, he would then give it to his slave. And it is said this applies to the slave who takes care of animals; and it is said in that and others, and that is more correct, because even if he does not always entrust to him, he does so sometimes. And if he returned it with a stranger, he is liable. And this issue indicates that the borrower does not have the right to subdeposit (ibda').
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage covers two related issues: first, returning a borrowed animal to the owner's stable. By strict analogy (qiyas), the borrower should be liable if the animal perishes after being left at the stable but before actual delivery to the owner. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), the borrower is not liable because this is the customary way to return borrowed animals. The other schools (Shafi'i, Hanbali, Maliki) follow qiyas. Hanafi follows istihsan based on custom. An exception is noted: if the stable is outside the house, the borrower remains liable because then leaving the animal there is not customary. Second, if the borrower returns the animal via his own slave or long-term hired worker (by year or month), he is not liable because they are considered trustworthy members of his household, similar to wadi'ah. If he uses a daily wage worker, he is liable because such a worker is not in his household. Also, if he returns via the owner's slave or worker, he is not liable because the owner implicitly consents. But if he gives it to a stranger, he is liable. This also shows that a borrower cannot sub-deposit (ibda') the borrowed item.

Key Terms
مستحسن (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides strict analogy due to custom, necessity, or ease.
أجير مياومة (ajir miyaumah): A daily wage laborer, not considered part of the household for trust purposes.
إبداع (ibda'): Sub-deposit; entrusting a borrowed item to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, a borrower returns a borrowed horse to the owner's stable, which is located inside the owner's house compound. The horse dies before the owner retrieves it. Is the borrower liable?
A) Yes, because he did not hand the horse directly to the owner.
B) No, because returning to the stable is customary and istihsan applies.
C) Yes, because the horse died in the stable.
D) No, only if the owner explicitly asked for it back.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to uphold customary practice in returning borrowed items, overriding strict analogy, but only when the return is genuinely customary and the item ends up in the owner's control.

Expense of Returning Borrowed, Leased, and Usurped Items
Ruling on liability for return costs of three property types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who bears the return expense for borrowed, leased, and usurped items according to the Hanafi school, and explain the legal rationale (ta'lil) for each.

Core Idea
Return expense follows benefit and obligation: borrower pays, lessor pays, usurper pays.

This passage from the Hanafi matn clarifies a practical question: when an item must be returned, who pays for the transport or effort? The answer depends on the nature of possession. The borrower, who benefited, bears the cost. The lessee only enables return; the lessor bears the cost. The usurper, who wrongfully took, must restore at his own expense. Understanding these distinctions prevents unjust burden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The passage separately rules on: (1) borrowed item (ariyah), (2) leased item (ijarah), (3) usurped item (ghasb). For each, note who is liable for the return expense (ajrah).
The text uses 'ajrah' meaning wage or expense of physical return.
2
Analyze the rationale for each
For borrowing: expense on borrower because he took possession for his own benefit (manfa'at nafsihi). For lease: expense on lessor because lessee only must enable (tamkin wa takhliyah), not physically return; benefit of possession accrues to lessor. For usurpation: expense on usurper because he must undo harm (daf' al-darar) by restoring to owner's hand.
The reasoning is based on benefit (manfa'ah) and obligation (wajib).
3
Apply to a practical scenario
Consider a borrowed book, a rented car, and a stolen phone. Determine who pays to deliver each back to the owner. Verify your answers with the rules.
This reinforces the conceptual distinction between physical return and enabling repossession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَ أَجْرَةُ رَدِ الْعَارِيَةِ عَلَى الْمُسْتَعِيرِ لاَنَّ الرَّدَّ وَاجِبٌ عَلَيْهِ لِمَا أَنَّهُ قَبَضَهُ لِمَنْفَعَةِ نَفْسِهِ وَالْآخِرَةُ مُؤْنَةِ الرَّةِ فَتَكُونُ عَلَيْهِ وَأَجْرَةُ رَةِ الْعَيْنِ الْمُسْتَاجَرَةِ عَلَى الْمُوَاجِرِ لِاَنَّ الْوَاجِبَ عَلَى الْمُسْتَاجِرِ التَّمْكِينَ وَالتَّخْلِيَةُ دُونَ الرَّةِ فَإِنَّ مَنْفَعَةَ قَبْضِهِ سَالِمَةٌ لِلْمُوَاجِرٍ مَعْنَى فَلَا يَكُونُ عَلَيْهِ مُؤْنَةٌ رَدِهِ وَأَجْرَةُ رَةِ الْعَيْنِ الْمَغْصُوبَةَ عَلَى الْعَاصِبِ لاَنَّ الْوَاجِبَ عَلَيْهِ الرَّدُّ وَالْإِعَادَةُ إِلى يَدِ الْمَالِكِ دَفْعًا لِلضَّرَرِ عَنْهُ فَيَكُونُ مُؤْنَتُهُ عَلَيْهِ
He said: The wage for returning a borrowed item is upon the borrower because the return is obligatory upon him, since he took possession for his own benefit, and the expense of return is a burden, so it is upon him. And the wage for returning a leased item is upon the lessor because what is obligatory upon the lessee is enabling and vacating, not returning, for the benefit of his possession is safe for the lessor in meaning, so the expense of returning is not upon him. And the wage for returning a usurped item is upon the usurper because what is obligatory upon him is the return and restoration into the hand of the owner to remove harm from him, so the expense is upon him.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on who bears the cost (ajrah) of returning property after a contract or wrongful possession. Three categories are distinguished: (1) For a borrowed item (ariyah), the borrower (musta'ir) must pay the cost of returning it because he took possession for his own benefit and returning it is his obligation. (2) For a leased item (ijarah), the lessor (mu'jir) pays the return cost because the lessee's obligation is only to enable the lessor to take possession again (tamkin wa takhliyah), not to physically return the item. The benefit of the lessee's possession ultimately accrues to the lessor (through rent). (3) For a usurped item (ghasb), the usurper (ghasib) pays the return cost because he must undo his wrongful act by restoring the item to the owner's hand. The reasoning is rational (ta'lil) based on the nature of each possession. No textual proofs are cited here, but the rulings are consistent with Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
أَجْرَةُ رَدِ الْعَارِيَةِ (ajratu raddi al-'āriyah): The wage or expense incurred in returning a borrowed item to its owner.
الْمُسْتَعِيرُ (al-musta'īr): The person who borrows (the borrower) an item for use without rent.
الْمُؤْجِرُ (al-mu'jir): The lessor or owner who leases out an item for rent.
الْغَاصِبُ (al-ghāṣib): The usurper who wrongfully takes possession of another's property.
التَّمْكِينُ وَالتَّخْلِيَةُ (al-tamkīn wa al-takhliyah): Enabling and vacating: allowing the owner to take possession without physically returning the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who bears the expense of returning a borrowed animal to its owner?
A) The borrower
B) The lender
C) The usurper
D) The lessor

Key Takeaway
Return expense is borne by the party who benefits from possession or is responsible for the wrong: borrower pays for ariyah, lessor pays for ijarah, usurper pays for ghasb.

intermediate
8 min
Returning Borrowed Items to Owner's Premises: Liability Discharge by Custom
Explaining when returning a borrowed item to the owner's home suffices to discharge liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that returning a borrowed item to the owner's customary place discharges liability, and distinguish this from the rulings on usurped items and deposits.

Core Idea
Custom makes a borrower safe by leaving a borrowed item at the owner's home – but not for usurped or deposited items.

This passage explains when returning a borrowed item to the owner's house or stable suffices to free the borrower from liability. The Hanafi school invokes istihsan (juristic preference) to override strict analogy (qiyas), relying on customary practice (urf). You will learn the rule, its rationale, and the important exceptions for usurped property (ghasb) and deposits (wadi'ah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the legal reasoning
Locate the Hanafi position: the borrower is not liable (la yadman) if he returns the animal to the owner's stable. Note that this is based on istihsan, not qiyas.
The passage states: 'هذا استحسان وفي القياس يضمن' – this is istihsan, by qiyas he would be liable.
2
Understand the basis of istihsan: custom (urf)
See that the justification is the customary delivery (التسليم المتعارف). The analogy to household items shows that returning to the house is the norm.
The owner himself would put the animal in the stable, so the borrower's action effectively completes the return.
3
Contrast with stricter contracts: ghasb and wadi'ah
Read the exceptions: for usurped items (المغصوب) and deposits (الوديعة), returning to the house is not sufficient. The usurper must undo his wrongful act by personal return; the depositary must return to the owner because trust was personal.
The owner would not be satisfied with depositing his item to the house; otherwise he would have left it there himself.
4
Consider the limitation: valuable items without custom
Note the example of a string of jewels (عقد جوهر). Such valuable items must be returned directly to the lender because no custom exists to leave them at the house.
Custom is the decisive factor; without it, the strict qiyas applies.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Compare with the Shafi'i view: a Hanafi borrower is safe if he leaves the item at the owner's premises, while a Shafi'i borrower must ensure personal delivery.
This shows how a single difference in usul (istihsan vs qiyas) changes daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا اسْتَعَارَ دَابَّةٌ فَرَدَّهَا إِلى أَصْطَبَل مَالِكِهَا فَهَلَكَتْ لَمْ يَضْمَنْ وَهَذَا إِسْتِحْسَانُ وَفِي الْقِيَاسِ يَضْمَنُ لِأَنَّهُ مَا رَدَّهَا إِلى مَالِكِهَا بَلْ ضَيَّعَهَا وَجْهُ الإِسْتِحْسَان انه اتى بالتَّسْلِيمِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ لِأَنَّ رَدَّ الْعَوَارَى إلى دَارِ الْمَالِكِ مُعْتَادٌ كَالَةِ الْبَيْتِ تُعَارُ ثُمَّ تُرَدُّ إِلَى الدَّارِ وَلَوْ رَدَّهَا إِلَى الْمَالِكِ فَالْمَالِكِ يَرُدُّهَا إِلَى الْمَرْبَطِ فَصَحَ رَدَّهُ وَإِنِ اسْتَعَارَ عَبْدًا فَرَدَّهُ إِلَى دَارِ الْمَالِكِ وَلَمْ يُسَلِمُهُ إِلَيْهِ لَمْ يَضْمَنْ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا وَلَوْ رَدَّ الْمَعْصُوبِ أَوِ الْوَدِيعَةَ إِلى دَارِ الْمَالِكِ وَلَمْ يُسَلَّمَ إِلَيْهِ ضَمِنَ لِاَنَّ الْوَاجِبَ عَلَى الْغَاصِبِ فَسْخُ فِعْلِهِ وَذَالِكَ بِالرَّةِ إِلَى الْمَالِكِ دُونَ غَيْرِهِ وَالْوَدِيْعَةُ لَا يَرْضَى الْمَالِكُ بَرَدِهَا إِلَى الدَّارِ وَلَا إِلى يَدِ مِنْ فِي الْعِيَالِ لِأَنَّهُ لَوِ ارْتَضَاهُ لِمَا أَوْ دَعَهَا إِيَّاهُ بِخِلَافِ الْعَوَارِى لِاَنَّ فِيهَا عُرُفًا حَتَّى لَوْ كَانَتِ الْعَارِيَةُ عِقْدَ جَوْهَرٍ لَمْ يَرُدَّهَا إِلَّا إِلَى الْمُعِيرِ لِعَدْمِ مَا ذَكَرْنَا مِنَ الْعُرْفِ فِيهِ
He said: And if one borrows an animal and returns it to its owner's stable and it perishes, he does not become liable. This is istihsan. By qiyas, he should be liable because he did not return it to its owner but rather destroyed it. The basis of istihsan is that he performed the customary delivery because returning borrowed items to the owner's house is customary, like household items are borrowed and then returned to the house. And if he returned it to the owner, the owner would return it to the stable, so his return is valid. And if he borrows a slave and returns him to the owner's house without handing him over to the owner, he does not become liable for what we explained. And if he returns a usurped item or a deposit to the owner's house without handing it over to him, he becomes liable because what is obligatory upon the usurper is to undo his action, and that is by returning to the owner, not to others. And the owner is not satisfied with the return of a deposit to the house nor to the hand of someone in his household, because if he were satisfied, he would not have deposited it with him. This is contrary to borrowed items because there is a custom regarding them, such that if the borrowed item is a string of jewels, he only returns it to the lender due to the absence of the custom we mentioned.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of returning borrowed items by leaving them at the owner's premises without directly handing them to the owner. The Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas. By qiyas, the borrower remains liable until the owner personally receives the item. However, by istihsan, returning to the owner's stable or house is sufficient if it is customary (urf). The reason is that the owner himself would put the animal in the stable, so the borrower's action effectively accomplishes the return. Similarly, returning a borrowed slave to the owner's house without handing him over is sufficient. However, this does not apply to usurped items (ghasb) or deposits (wadi'ah). For an usurped item, the usurper must personally return it to the owner because his obligation is to reverse his wrongful act. For a deposit, returning to the house is not sufficient because the owner's trust was in the depositary personally, not in his home. Furthermore, the custom of leaving borrowed items at the home applies only to ordinary items; valuable items like a necklace of jewels must be returned directly to the lender because no such custom exists.

Key Terms
اصطبل (iṣṭabl): Stable; the place where the owner keeps his animal.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; departing from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more suitable ruling based on custom, necessity, or equity.
قِيَاس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a common effective cause ('illa).
عرف (ʿurf): Custom; established practice in a community.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; wrongful possession of another's property.
وديعة (wadīʿah): Deposit; property entrusted to another for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a borrower returns a borrowed horse to the owner's stable and the horse dies, is the borrower liable?
A) Yes, by qiyas
B) No, by istihsan
C) Yes, always
D) Only if the stable was locked

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, custom (urf) allows a borrower to discharge liability by returning borrowed items to the owner's premises, but this does not apply to usurped or deposited items, nor to valuable items where no custom exists.

Borrowing Land for Cultivation: Owner Cannot Reclaim Until Harvest
Commentary on a ruling about borrowing land for cultivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on borrowing land for cultivation and explain the rationale behind the prohibition on premature revocation.

Core Idea
Once land is borrowed and cultivated, the owner cannot reclaim it until harvest — the borrower's right supersedes the owner's right to revoke.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding aariyah (gratuitous loan) of land for cultivation. While aariyah is normally revocable at will, when the land has been sown, the owner's right to take back the land is suspended until the crop is harvested. The reasoning balances the borrower's investment with the owner's property rights by allowing an equivalent rent (ajr al-mithl). We'll explore the ruling, its rationale, and the exception for trees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is borrowing land specifically for cultivation. The ruling: the owner cannot reclaim until the harvest is complete.
This applies whether or not a time was specified.
2
Understand the condition
The condition that triggers the ruling: the land must have been actually cultivated (sown by the borrower).
If no cultivation has taken place, the general rules of aariyah (revocable at will) apply.
3
Analyze the rationale
The harvest has a known end, so allowing the owner to reclaim mid-season would cause loss to the borrower. The owner is compensated by ajr al-mithl for the period.
4
Distinguish the exception
Trees have no known harvest time, so the owner can order their uprooting at any time, unlike crops.
This is because trees are considered to have no fixed lifespan.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a farmer who borrows land, sows wheat, and then the owner demands the land back. Apply the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولو استعار ليذر عها.. الخ. اور اگر زمین کو اسی غرض سے مستعار لیا کہ اس میں کاشت کرے تو مالک زمین کو اپنی زمین واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ یہاں تک کہ کھیتی کٹ جائے خواہ وقت مقرر کیا ہو یا نہ کیا ہو۔ اس لئے کہ کھیتی کٹنے کی ایک انتہاء معلوم ہے اور اجر مثل پر مستغیر کے پاس چھوڑنے میں دونوں کے حق کی رعایت ہے " بخلاف درختوں کے۔ کہ ان کی کوئی انتہاء معلوم نہیں تو ان کے اکھاڑنے کا حکم کیا جائے گا۔
His statement: 'And if he borrows to sow it...' etc. And if land is borrowed with the intention of cultivating it, the owner of the land will not have the right to take back his land until the crop is harvested, whether a time was specified or not. Because the harvest has a known end, and leaving it with the borrower for an equivalent rent takes into account the right of both. Unlike trees, because they have no known end, so the order to uproot them will be given.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling from Al-Hidayah. It explains the ruling on borrowing land specifically for cultivation. The key point is that once the borrower has sown the land, the owner cannot evict him arbitrarily. The reason is that the crop has a fixed term until harvest, and allowing the owner to take back the land mid-season would cause loss to the borrower. The owner can demand an equivalent rent (ajr al-mithl) for the period the land remains with the borrower after the original permission. This is a Hanafi ruling that balances the rights of both parties. The exception is trees, which have no fixed harvest time, so the owner can demand their removal at any time.

Key Terms
عارضیت (aariyah): A contract of gratuitous loan where possession is transferred for use without compensation.
مستعار (musta'ar): The person who borrows the item (borrower).
اجر مثل (ajr al-mithl): The equivalent market rent for the use of the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if land is borrowed for cultivation, when can the owner reclaim it?
A) At any time
B) After the harvest is complete
C) Only if a time was specified
D) After paying compensation to the borrower

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, borrowing land for cultivation gives the borrower a right to keep it until harvest, with the owner compensated by ajr al-mithl, unlike trees which can be ordered removed at any time.

Expense of Returning Borrowed Item and Valid Return to Stable
Rulings on who bears return expense and valid return methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who bears the expense of returning borrowed, rented, and usurped items, and explain why returning a borrowed animal to the owner's stable is valid by istihsan.

Core Idea
The burden of return falls on the party who benefits or caused harm; custom validates delivery to the place of custody.

This lesson examines two key rulings from Al-Hidayah. First, the allocation of return expenses for borrowed, rented, and usurped items. Second, the validity of returning a borrowed animal to the owner's stable. Understanding these rulings clarifies the obligations of possession and the role of custom in defining valid delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of items
The text discusses borrowed items ('ariyah), rented items (musatajarah), and usurped items (maghsubah). For each, identify who bears the expense of returning them.
Borrowed: expense on borrower. Rented: expense on lessor. Usurped: expense on usurper.
2
Analyze the rationale for each expense
For borrowed items, the borrower benefits from the item, so return expense is on him. For rented, the lessor retains ultimate benefit and the lessee only vacates. For usurped, the usurper must remove harm from the owner.
3
Examine the second ruling: return to stable
The borrower returns a borrowed beast to the owner's stable, not to the owner personally. If it perishes before the owner retrieves it, is the borrower liable?
4
Understand qiyas vs istihsan
By qiyas (strict analogy), the borrower would be liable because he didn't return it to the owner's hand. By istihsan, he is not liable because he performed delivery according to custom ('urf).
Istihsan overrides qiyas here due to customary practice.
5
Recognize the Hanafi preference for istihsan
The Hanafi school prioritizes istihsan (juridical preference) over qiyas when custom or necessity dictates. This ruling exemplifies that method.
Compare with returning borrowed household items to the house.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَ أَجْرَةُ رَدِ الْعَارِيَةِ عَلَى الْمُسْتَعِيرِ لاَنَّ الرَّدَّ وَاجِبٌ عَلَيْهِ لِمَا أَنَّهُ قَبَضَهُ لِمَنْفَعَةِ نَفْسِهِ وَالْآخِرَةُ مُؤْنَةِ الرَّةِ فَتَكُونُ عَلَيْهِ وَأَجْرَةُ رَةِ الْعَيْنِ الْمُسْتَاجَرَةِ عَلَى الْمُوَاجِرِ لِاَنَّ الْوَاجِبَ عَلَى الْمُسْتَاجِرِ التَّمْكِينَ وَالتَّخْلِيَةُ دُونَ الرَّةِ فَإِنَّ مَنْفَعَةَ قَبْضِهِ سَالِمَةٌ لِلْمُوَاجِرٍ مَعْنَى فَلَا يَكُونُ عَلَيْهِ مُؤْنَةٌ رَدِهِ وَأَجْرَةُ رَةِ الْعَيْنِ الْمَغْصُوبَةَ عَلَى الْعَاصِبِ لاَنَّ الْوَاجِبَ عَلَيْهِ الرَّدُّ وَالْإِعَادَةُ إِلى يَدِ الْمَالِكِ دَفْعًا لِلضَّرَرِ عَنْهُ فَيَكُونُ مُؤْنَتُهُ عَلَيْهِ قَالَ وَإِذَا اسْتَعَارَ دَابَّةٌ فَرَدَّهَا إِلى أَصْطَبَل مَالِكِهَا فَهَلَكَتْ لَمْ يَضْمَنْ وَهَذَا إِسْتِحْسَانُ وَفِي الْقِيَاسِ يَضْمَنُ لِأَنَّهُ مَا رَدَّهَا إِلى مَالِكِهَا بَلْ ضَيَّعَهَا وَجْهُ الإِسْتِحْسَان انه اتى بالتَّسْلِيمِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ لِأَنَّ رَدَّ الْعَوَارَى إلى دَارِ الْمَالِكِ مُعْتَادٌ كَالَةِ الْبَيْتِ تُعَارُ ثُمَّ تُرَدُّ إِلَى الدَّارِ وَلَوْ رَدَّهَا إِلَى الْمَالِكِ فَالْمَالِكِ يَرُدُّهَا إِلَى الْمَرْبَطِ فَصَحَ رَدَّهُ
He said: The expense of returning the borrowed item is upon the borrower, because returning it is obligatory upon him, since he took possession for his own benefit, and the expense of returning is a burden, so it is upon him. And the expense of returning a rented item is upon the lessor, because what is obligatory upon the lessee is enabling and vacating, not returning; for the benefit of his possession is preserved for the lessor in meaning, so the burden of returning is not upon him. And the expense of returning a usurped item is upon the usurper, because what is obligatory upon him is returning and restoring to the owner's hand to remove harm from him, so its burden is upon him. He said: And if one borrows a beast and returns it to the owner's stable and it perishes, he is not liable. This is istihsan. By qiyas, he would be liable because he did not return it to the owner but rather caused its loss. The basis of istihsan is that he performed delivery according to custom, because returning borrowed items to the owner's house is customary, like household utensils are borrowed then returned to the house. And if he returned it to the owner, the owner would return it to the stable, so his return is valid.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a matn from Al-Hidayah with commentary. It covers two rulings: (1) Who bears the cost of returning borrowed, rented, and usurped items, and (2) the validity of returning a borrowed animal to the owner's stable. For borrowed items, the borrower must pay for return because he took possession for his own benefit. For rented items, the lessor bears the cost because the lessee only needs to vacate. For usurped items, the usurper bears the cost because he must restore to the owner's hand. Then, returning a borrowed animal to the owner's stable is sufficient delivery by custom (istihsan), even though by qiyas it would not be a proper return. The Hanafi school prefers istihsan here because of customary practice, similar to returning borrowed household items to the house.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference, deviating from qiyas for a stronger reason like custom or necessity.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning, here the strict analogy that would impose liability.
أصطبل (istabl): Stable, the place where the animal is normally kept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who bears the cost of returning a borrowed item?
A) The owner
B) The borrower
C) Both equally
D) The state

Key Takeaway
The expense of return is borne by the borrower for loans, lessor for rentals, and usurper for usurpation; returning to the owner's stable is valid by custom.

Wording of Contract for Borrowing Land: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on contract wording for land loan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the contractual wording for borrowing land for cultivation and explain the basis of each argument.

Core Idea
Specific wording ('at'am') vs standard term ('a'ara'): a dispute over precision vs consistency in contract language.

Precision in contract wording is crucial in Islamic law, as it determines the scope and conditions of an agreement. This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the preferred wording when documenting a loan of land for cultivation. The dispute highlights a deeper tension between using terms that clearly indicate the intended purpose and adhering to established contractual language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed element
The act is documenting a loan (i'arah) of barren land for cultivation. The disputed element is the specific verb to write in the contract: 'at'am' (you fed me) or 'a'ara' (you lent me).
The contract itself is valid with either wording.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that the writer should use 'at'amtani' (you fed me). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the writer should use 'a'rtani' (you lent me).
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
Abu Hanifa argues that 'at'am' is more specific to cultivation, and since the purpose is cultivation, the term that best expresses that purpose is preferable. 'I'arah' is general and could include building or other uses.
This reflects a focus on clarity of intention (maqasid).
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's dalil
The Sahibayn argue that 'i'arah' (loan) is the standardized term for loan contracts, and using the established contractual word is better, just as a house loan contract uses 'i'arah' without alternative.
This emphasizes consistency and clarity of contractual terminology.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference is minor: both wordings create a valid contract, but the choice clarifies the purpose. Later Hanafi scholars generally prefer the Sahibayn's view for consistency, making it the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ أَعَارَ أَرْضًا بَيْضَاءَ لِلزَّرَاعَةِ يَكْتُبْ إِنَّكَ أَطْعَمْتَنِي عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا يَكْتُبُ انَّكَ أَرْتَنِي لأَنَّ لَفْظَةَ الْإِعَارَةِ مَوْضُوعَةٌ لَهُ وَالْكِتَابَةُ بِالْمَوْضُوع أَوْلى كَمَا فِي إِعَارَةِ الدَّارِ وَلَهُ أَنَّ لَفْظَةَ الْإِطْعَامِ ادَلُ عَلَى الْمُرَادِ لِأَنَّهَا تَخْتَصُّ بِالزَّرَاعَةِ وَالْإِعَارَةُ يَنْتَظِمُهَا وَغَيْرَهَا كَالْبِنَاءِ وَنَحْوِهِ فَكَانَتِ الْكِتَابَةُ بِهَا أَوْلَى بِخِلَافِ الدَّارِ لأَنَّهَا لَا تُعَارُ إِلَّا لِلسُّكْنَى
He said: And whoever lends a barren land for cultivation should write: 'You have fed me (at'amtani)' according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He should write: 'You have lent me (a'rtani)' because the word 'i'arah' (loan) is specifically for that, and writing with the specific word is better, as in the loan of a house. And for him (Abu Hanifa), the word 'it'am' (feeding) is more indicative of the intended meaning because it is specific to cultivation, while 'i'arah' covers it and also other things like building, etc., so writing with it is better, unlike a house because it is only lent for dwelling.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the wording of a contract for borrowing land for cultivation. The issue: when documenting the loan, what term should be used? Abu Hanifa prefers 'at'am' (you fed me) because it is specific to cultivation, which is the purpose here. 'I'arah' is more general and could apply to building, etc. The Sahibayn prefer 'a'ara' (you lent to me) because it is the proper term for a loan contract, and it is better to use the actual contractual term. They analogize to the loan of a house, where only 'i'arah' is used. The practical difference is minimal but reflects a deeper methodological difference: Abu Hanifa focuses on clarity of purpose, while the Sahibayn emphasize consistency of contractual language. The relied-upon position is the Sahibayn's view, as it matches the standard doctrine.

Key Terms
اطعمتني (at'amtani): Literally 'you fed me', used metaphorically for giving land for cultivation.
أعرتني (a'rtani): You lent to me, the standard word for a loan contract.
ارض بيضاء (ard bayda'): Barren or uncultivated land suitable for farming.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what word should be used in a contract for borrowing land for cultivation?
A) أعرتني (a'rtani)
B) أطعمتني (at'amtani)
C) أسكنتني (askantani)
D) وهبتني (wahabtani)

Key Takeaway
In drafting contracts for borrowing land for cultivation, the Sahibayn's view to use standard loan terminology ('a'ara') is the operative position, though Abu Hanifa's emphasis on specifying the purpose has merit and can be accommodated by including a purpose clause.

8 min
Returning a Borrowed Animal via Owner's Slave or Servant
Explains liability when borrower returns animal to owner's household

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning a borrowed animal to the owner's household, distinguish between regular and daily-wage servants, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of slaves.

Core Idea
Returning to the owner's household removes liability; household includes regular servants, not daily-wage workers.

This lesson explores a practical scenario in the contract of 'āriyah (loan for use): the borrower returns the borrowed animal not to the owner personally but to the owner's slave or servant. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling based on the principle that the owner's household is an extension of the owner. We will unpack the conditions, the analogical proof from Wadi'ah, and an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the scope of slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Condition
State the basic ruling: returning the borrowed animal to the owner's slave or regular servant removes liability. Note the key condition: the servant must be part of the owner's household (yearly or monthly hire, not daily-wage).
The text explicitly excludes the daily-wage servant (ajir yawmiyyah).
2
Understand the Analogical Proof (Qiyas)
The ruling is derived by analogy (qiyas) to Wadi'ah (deposit). Just as a depositor can hand the deposit to a family member without liability, a borrower can return the borrowed item to the owner's household.
The common illa: both are amanat-holders and the household is implicitly trusted.
3
Distinguish Between Types of Servants
The 'ajir khas (yearly/monthly) is considered part of the household ('iyal), so returning to him is valid. The daily-wage servant is not part of the household; hence liability remains.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
4
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Slaves
Some scholars limit the ruling to slaves who are grooms (sa'is); others extend it to all slaves. The text prefers the broader view (ashah) because the owner occasionally entrusts any slave with the animal.
The mufta bihi is the broader view, per Al-Marghinani.
5
Extend to the Deposit Issue
The passage also addresses whether the borrower can give the borrowed item as a deposit (Wadi'ah) to a third party. The relied-upon view permits it, as giving a deposit is less than giving a loan.
The prohibition mentioned in some sources applies only after the loan period ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فردها مع عبد الخ
ترجمہ کسی نے چوپایہ مستعار لیا اور اس کو اپنے غلام یا نوکر کے ہاتھ واپس کیا تو ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ اجر سے مراد وہ ہے جو سالانہ یاماہانہ نوکری پر ہو۔ کیونکہ وہ امانت ہے تو وہ اس سے حفاظت کر سکتا ہے جو اس کی عیال میں ہے۔ جیسے ودیعت میں ہوتا ہے بخلاف یومیہ اجیر کے کیوں کہ وہ اس کی عیال میں نہیں ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر اس کو مالک کے غلام یا نوکر کے ہاتھ واپس کیا۔ کیونکہ مالک اس سے راضی ہے کیا نہیں دیکھتے کہ اگر وہ مالک کو واپس کے تو مالک اپنے غلام کودے گا۔ اور کہا گیا ہے کہ یہ اس غلام کی بابت ہے جو چو پاؤں کی دیکھ بھال کرتا ہے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ سب میں یہی حکم ہے اور یہی اصح ہے اس لئے کہ مالک اگر اس کو ہمیشہ نہیں دیا تو کبھی کبھی تو دیتا ہے۔ اور اگر کسی اجنبی کے ہاتھ واپس کیا تو ضامن ہوگا۔ اور مسئلہ اس پر دال ہے کہ مستعغیر قصد اود یعت نہیں دے سکتا۔ جیسا کہ بعض مشائخ نے کہنا ہے اور بعض نے کہا ہے کہ دے سکتا ہے کیونکہ ودیعت دینا عاربیت دینے سے کم ہے اور انہوں نے اس مسئلہ کی تاویل مدت گزرنے سے عاریت ختم ہو جانے کے ساتھ کی ہے۔
His statement: 'He returned it with a slave etc.': Translation: Someone borrowed an animal and returned it via his own slave or servant; he will not be liable. 'Ajeer' here means one hired on a yearly or monthly basis, because that person is an Amanat-holder and can entrust it to someone in his household, like in Wadi'ah. Unlike a daily-wage servant, who is not in his household. Similarly, if he returned it to the owner's slave or servant, because the owner is satisfied with that; do you not see that if he returns it to the owner, the owner will give it to his slave? It is said that this applies to the slave who tends to the animal, and it is said the same ruling applies to all slaves, and that is more correct because even if the owner does not always give it to a non-groom slave, sometimes he does. If he returns it to a stranger, he will be liable. This matter indicates that the borrower cannot intentionally give the borrowed item as a deposit. Some shaykhs say he cannot, others say he can because giving a deposit is less than giving a loan, and they interpret this case as when the loan period has ended so the loan is terminated.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the liability of a borrower when returning a borrowed animal to the owner's slave or servant. The ruling: the borrower is not liable if he returns the animal to the owner's slave or a servant who is part of the owner's household (e.g., a yearly/monthly servant). The proof is analogical (qiyas) to Wadi'ah (deposit): just as a depositor can hand the deposit to a family member, a borrower can return the borrowed item to the owner's household. The text distinguishes between a regular servant ('ajir) who lives with the owner and a daily-wage servant (yawmiyyah) who is not part of the household; returning to the latter would make the borrower liable. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some shaykhs limit this ruling to slaves who are grooms (sa'is), while others extend it to all slaves; the second view is deemed more correct (ashah). The passage also discusses the borrower's ability to give the borrowed item as a deposit (wadi'ah) to a third party: some shaykhs (e.g., Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi) forbid it, while others (e.g., Abu al-Layth al-Bukhari, Imam Muhammad) permit it. The latter is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) view. The apparent contradiction is resolved by interpreting the prohibition as applying when the loan period has expired.

Key Terms
مستعير (musta'ir): The person who borrows an item (borrower) in a contract of 'āriyah (loan for use).
أجير (ajīr): A hired servant; here distinguished between yearly/monthly (ajīr khāṣṣ) and daily-wage (ajīr 'āmm).
عارية ('āriyah): A loan for use without compensation, a contract of gratuitous use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because the servant is part of the owner's household.
B) Yes, because the daily-wage servant is not considered part of the owner's household.
C) Yes, because the borrower can only return to the owner himself.
D) No, because all servants are considered the same.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, returning a borrowed item to the owner's household (including his regular servants and slaves) absolves the borrower of liability, as the household is considered an extension of the owner.

Wording of the Loan Contract: 'At'amtanī' vs 'A'artanī'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on recommended contract wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the wording of a loan contract for agricultural land and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Specific wording avoids ambiguity; generality invites legal disputes.

When lending land for cultivation, the precise wording of the contract matters. This lesson examines a famous intra-Hanafi dispute over whether to use 'at'amtanī' (you fed me) or 'a'artanī' (you lent me) in the loan document. The dispute is not about the validity of the contract but about the recommended form for clarity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is lending bare land for cultivation. The dispute is about what wording the lender should write in the contract: 'at'amtanī' (you fed me) or 'a'artanī' (you lent me).
The land is empty (arḍ bayḍā') and specifically for farming.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa says to write 'at'amtanī' because it specifically indicates the purpose of cultivation, avoiding ambiguity that could arise from the general term 'i'ārah' which includes other uses like building.
He argues that the specific term (khāṣṣ) is better than the general (ʿāmm) when the intended use is clear.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say to write 'a'artanī' because 'i'ārah' is the legal term for a loan contract, and using the designated term (mawḍūʿ) is preferable. They analogize to the loan of a house, where only 'a'artanī' is used.
Their qiyas: just as a house has a single typical use (dwelling) so no ambiguity, land for cultivation is also a single intended use, so the general term suffices.
4
Analyze the rebuttal and determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Abu Hanifa responds that a house is different because it is only lent for dwelling, whereas land can be lent for building, planting, or other purposes, so specificity is beneficial. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is minimal: both wordings are valid, but the chosen wording affects clarity and potential future disputes.
The later fatwa generally prefers the Sahibayn's view, but Abu Hanifa's view is also acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ومن أعار أرضًا بيضاء للزراعة يكتب إنك أطعمتني عند أبي حنيفة وقالا يكتب إنك أعرتني لأن لفظة الإعارة موضوعة له والكتابة بالموضوع أولى كما في إعارة الدار وله أن لفظة الإطعام أدل على المراد لأنها تختص بالزراعة والإعارة ينتظمها وغيرها كالبناء ونحوه فكانت الكتابة بها أولى بخلاف الدار لأنها لا تعار إلا للسكنى والله أعلم بالصواب
ترجمہ جس نے خالی زمین عاریت دی کاشت کے لئے تو لکھے کہ تو نے مجھے کھانے کے لئے دی ہے امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کیہ یہ لکھے کہ تو نے مجھے عاریت دہی ہے کیونکہ لفظ اعادہ اس کے لئے موضوع ہے اور موضوع کے ساتھ تحریر کرنا بہتر ہے جیسے مکان کے اعادہ میں ہوتا ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ الطعام مراد پر زیادہ دلالت کرتا ہے کیونکہ یہ شخص بزراعت ہے اور اعادہ اس کو اور اس کے علاوہ عمارت وغیرہ کو شامل ہے پس لفظ مختص کا لکھنا بہتر ہے، بخلاف مکان کے۔ کیونکہ وہ رہائش ہی کے لئے عاریت لیا جاتا ہے۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
His statement: 'And whoever lends a bare land for cultivation, he should write: you fed me, according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: he should write: you lent me, because the word 'i'ārah' is legally designated for this, and writing with what is designated is better, as in the loan of a house. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that the word 'iṭ'ām' indicates the intended meaning more specifically because it is exclusive to cultivation, whereas 'i'ārah' includes both cultivation and other things like construction etc. So writing with the specific term is better, unlike a house, because a house is only lent for dwelling. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn) regarding the proper wording to use in a written loan contract (document) when lending land for cultivation. The issue is not about the validity of the loan but about the recommended form of writing to avoid ambiguity. Abu Hanifa holds that the lender should write 'at'amtani' (you fed me) because the purpose is cultivation to produce food, and this specific word makes the intent clear. The Sahibayn argue that since the contract is a loan (i'ārah), the proper word is 'a'artani' (you lent me), as this is the legal term for the contract. They analogize to the loan of a house, where 'a'artani' is used without dispute. Abu Hanifa responds that 'at'amtani' is more specific to cultivation, whereas 'a'artani' is general and could include building or other uses; thus using the specific term is better to indicate the exact purpose. The exception is a house, because it is only lent for dwelling, so there is no ambiguity. The text does not explicitly state which view is mufta bihi, but the placement in Al-Hidayah suggests the Sahibayn's view is considered stronger; however, practical fatwa may vary.

Key Terms
أطعمتني (aṭ'amtanī): Literally 'you fed me'; used metaphorically in the context of lending land for cultivation.
أعرتني (a'artanī): 'You lent me'; the standard term for a loan contract (i'ārah).
أرض بيضاء (arḍ bayḍā'): 'White land' meaning empty/unplanted land, ready for cultivation.

Key Terms from Source Passage
